    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1426383483337653137">Like much of G. K. Chesterton's fiction, <i>The Ball and the Cross</i> is both witty and profound, cloaking serious religious and philosophical inquiry in sparkling humor and whimsy. Serialized in the British publication <i>The Commonwealth</i> in 1905-06, Chesterton's second novel first appeared in book form in America in 1909, delighting and challenging readers with its heady mixture </span>
  <span id="freeText1426383483337653137" style="display:none">Like much of G. K. Chesterton's fiction, <i>The Ball and the Cross</i> is both witty and profound, cloaking serious religious and philosophical inquiry in sparkling humor and whimsy. Serialized in the British publication <i>The Commonwealth</i> in 1905-06, Chesterton's second novel first appeared in book form in America in 1909, delighting and challenging readers with its heady mixture of fantasy, farce, and theology.<br />The plot of <i>The Ball and the Cross </i>chronicles a hot dispute between two Scotsmen, one a devout but naive Roman Catholic, the other a zealous but naive atheist. Their fanatically held opinions—leading to a duel that is proposed but never fought—inspire a host of comic adventures whose allegorical levels vigorously explore the debate between theism and atheism.<br />Martin Gardner's superb introduction to <i>The Ball and the Cross</i> reveals the real-life debate between Chesterton and a famous atheist that provided inspiration for the story, and it explores some of the novel's possible allegorical meanings. Appraising the book's many intriguing philosophical qualities, Mr. Gardner alerts readers as well to the pleasures of its "colorful style . . . amusing puns and clever paradoxes . . . and the humor and melodrama of its crazy plot."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1426383483337653137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div id="review_669787201" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/669787201" />
    <a title="Coop Williams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20134650-coop-williams"><img alt="Coop Williams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566229411p2/20134650.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/669787201?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Coop Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Coop Williams" href="/user/show/20134650-coop-williams">Coop Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20134650-coop-williams?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer669787201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer495704909981309112">My second Chesterton work has awakened in me a most wonderful kind of rage. It is the rage that drives a fervent Catholic to hurl a rock through the window of an editorial office. It is the rage with which an atheist prints blasphemy and logical syllogisms. The rage by which both men take up swords time and again to defend their views.<br/><br/>On the other hand, Chesterton's gentlemanly prose exudes forgiveness. Similarly to The Man Who Was Thursday, the author paints a picture of the cosmos's workings t</span>
  <span id="freeText495704909981309112" style="display:none">My second Chesterton work has awakened in me a most wonderful kind of rage. It is the rage that drives a fervent Catholic to hurl a rock through the window of an editorial office. It is the rage with which an atheist prints blasphemy and logical syllogisms. The rage by which both men take up swords time and again to defend their views.<br /><br />On the other hand, Chesterton's gentlemanly prose exudes forgiveness. Similarly to The Man Who Was Thursday, the author paints a picture of the cosmos's workings through the people who inhabit it. It is a picture of both hysterical structure and collected contradiction. The war between the ball and the cross is never biased, but always dynamic. I've never seen so strange a friendship in a book as the polemic bond between the two heroes. A mystic reader may be surprised by admiration of Turnbull's clever and earnest personality. The skeptic may find himself overtaken by MacIan's assurance and his support from the natural world. <br /><br />The "unaffiliated" reader may be struck most profoundly, or not at all. The book is less a response to atheism than it is to fat-minded complacency. I mourn for those who have neutered their minds and actions, the lukewarm. The deluded and distracted are truly the most lost, and it might take a shock like this to awaken them. To awaken us, I should say. This book is a strikingly human document that progresses from madcap to metaphysical, and epitomizes the struggle between the material and the spiritual.</span>
  <a data-text-id="495704909981309112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440760649" />

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440760649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4136329538564702522">Chesterton’s novels almost stand in a genre of their own. Heavily philosophical, wildly allegorical, unapologetically adventurous, and comically surreal, it can be difficult even to describe them. And of them all, perhaps The Ball and the Cross is the most peculiar; which might be to say the worst, if you could even use a superlative negative in a sentence about Chesterton’s works. At least it does not operate on the same level of high genius as The Man Who Was Thursday. But what there is of it </span>
  <span id="freeText4136329538564702522" style="display:none">Chesterton’s novels almost stand in a genre of their own. Heavily philosophical, wildly allegorical, unapologetically adventurous, and comically surreal, it can be difficult even to describe them. And of them all, perhaps The Ball and the Cross is the most peculiar; which might be to say the worst, if you could even use a superlative negative in a sentence about Chesterton’s works. At least it does not operate on the same level of high genius as The Man Who Was Thursday. But what there is of it is unforgettable.<br /><br />Read the rest at my blog, <a href="http://inwhichireadvintagenovels.blogspot.com.au/2013/03/the-ball-and-cross-by-gk-chesterton.html" rel="nofollow noopener">In Which I Read Vintage Novels</a></span>

<div id="review_569487060" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/569487060" />
    <a title="Johan Haneveld" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18673428-johan-haneveld"><img alt="Johan Haneveld" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445600301p2/18673428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/569487060?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johan Haneveld" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johan Haneveld" href="/user/show/18673428-johan-haneveld">Johan Haneveld</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer569487060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8878612195667834740">Another great book by G.K. Chesterton. The more I read of him, the more I'm a fan. Yes, I do think Chesterton is in the 'hate it or love it' category, and I think one must have a taste for his pretty peculiar way of writing, but if one does, all of his works are like draughts from a spring of fresh, clear water. This one is no exception, filled to the brim with his trademark paradoxes and witticisms, coupling an alagorical story with lively descriptions and characters that feel fully alive, marr</span>
  <span id="freeText8878612195667834740" style="display:none">Another great book by G.K. Chesterton. The more I read of him, the more I'm a fan. Yes, I do think Chesterton is in the 'hate it or love it' category, and I think one must have a taste for his pretty peculiar way of writing, but if one does, all of his works are like draughts from a spring of fresh, clear water. This one is no exception, filled to the brim with his trademark paradoxes and witticisms, coupling an alagorical story with lively descriptions and characters that feel fully alive, marrying theological and philosphical speculation with an overwhelming love of life, giving one the impression of having been taken of a journey, more than just having read a book. Yes, this is unashamedly christian fiction, but it's also science fiction, and magical realism, with airships, and an apocalyptic conclusion. An atheist and a catholic seem to be the only ones left in the world to whom the question if God is real really means something, and they think their difference in opinion worth fighting about. The world does not think so and they are hindered at every point. Eventually though, even though they are saner than the people around them, they find they were also wrong: they must reach a position not of fanatism, but of love: love of the peculiar, the personal, love of the other (as exemplified by the pretty weird figure of the priest). That this novel ends with a miracla and a conversion could be seen as a cop out, but it doesn't feel like it is. It fits with the apocalyptic nature of the last chapters, of waking up in stead of dreaming, and reminds me of the miracle I hope to experience at the end of my life or of history. When we will no longer dream, but enter the end of all things that is more real, more concrete, than the world which it ends.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8878612195667834740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1742430535" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1742430535" />
    <a title="Simon Stegall" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43116967-simon-stegall"><img alt="Simon Stegall" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562904765p2/43116967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1742430535?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Stegall" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Stegall" href="/user/show/43116967-simon-stegall">Simon Stegall</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1742430535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7953556411373233270">The Ball And the Cross is about two Scotchmen, an atheist and a Catholic, who, due to certain irreconcilable differences, decide to fight a duel to the death. Unfortunately dueling is against the law, so the two champions are forced to make a run for it across the English countryside, avoiding the authorities and various philosophical types that try to stop them, in search of a place where they can kill each other peacefully.<br/><br/>It's a fun book, like most of Chesterton's fiction, and it's also profo</span>
  <span id="freeText7953556411373233270" style="display:none">The Ball And the Cross is about two Scotchmen, an atheist and a Catholic, who, due to certain irreconcilable differences, decide to fight a duel to the death. Unfortunately dueling is against the law, so the two champions are forced to make a run for it across the English countryside, avoiding the authorities and various philosophical types that try to stop them, in search of a place where they can kill each other peacefully.<br /><br />It's a fun book, like most of Chesterton's fiction, and it's also profound, albeit a little heavy-handed on the symbolism (but we wouldn't read Chesterton if we didn't need a little of that). And after all the fun and various profundities, the feeling I am left with is the same from book to book when it comes to this author; I wish I understood Christianity the way that mirth-and-girth Gilbert did. To illustrate, I leave you with a quote:<br /><br />'MacIan turned upon the Tolstoyian with a white face and bitter lip. “Sir,” he said, “talk about the principle of love as much as you like. You seem to me colder than a lump of stone; but I am willing to believe that you may at some time have loved a cat, or a dog, or a child. When you were a baby, I suppose you loved your mother. Talk about love, then, till the world is sick of the word. But don’t you talk about Christianity. Don’t you dare to say one word, white or black, about it. Christianity is, as far as you are concerned, a horrible mystery. Keep clear of it, keep silent upon it, as you would upon an abomination. It is a thing that has made men slay and torture each other; and you will never know why. It is a thing that has made men do evil that good might come; and you will never understand the evil, let alone the good. Christianity is a thing that could only make you vomit, till you are other than you are. I would not justify it to you even if I could. Hate it, in God’s name, as Turnbull does, who is a man. It is a monstrous thing, for which men die. And if you will stand here and talk about love for another ten minutes it is very probable that you will see a man die for it.”'<br /><br />Make of it what you will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7953556411373233270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_883919697" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/883919697" />
    <a title="Sandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2317252-sandra"><img alt="Sandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242272875p2/2317252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/883919697?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandra" href="/user/show/2317252-sandra">Sandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2317252-sandra?shelf=christian">christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2317252-sandra?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer883919697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13011794847220170830">Another Sci-Fi story from Chesterton, however much easier to follow than <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/184419.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday' title='The Man Who Was Thursday by G.K. Chesterton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Man Who Was Thursday</a>. A real duel with arms needs to take place between a Catholic and an atheist, they struggle to find the right place and so they become fugitives and comrades. Their adventures are both funny and witty and both of them start leaning towards his enemy.<br/><br/>Chesterton again surprised me by being able (in such a short book) to portray two opposite characters in such a way that the reader feels sympathy for b</span>
  <span id="freeText13011794847220170830" style="display:none">Another Sci-Fi story from Chesterton, however much easier to follow than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/184419.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday" title="The Man Who Was Thursday by G.K. Chesterton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Man Who Was Thursday</a>. A real duel with arms needs to take place between a Catholic and an atheist, they struggle to find the right place and so they become fugitives and comrades. Their adventures are both funny and witty and both of them start leaning towards his enemy.<br /><br />Chesterton again surprised me by being able (in such a short book) to portray two opposite characters in such a way that the reader feels sympathy for both of them. This story is not only a duel between theism and atheism, but an example of how any extreme would lead to a broken world. It also reminds the reader that common sense is what will save humanity. I loved that it makes fun out of some materialists who will take materialism over facts, even when the fact is standing in front of them. As Albert Einstein once said, "if the facts don't fit the theory, then change the facts"; in this book, Chesterton takes this mockery to the next level by implying that modern "science" (psychology in this case) would rather ignore all the facts and declare everyone crazy in order to convince others than something real doesn't exist (the duel between the main characters).<br /><br />I recommend this book because it provides good entertainment while explaining big philosophical, theological and rationalistic issues from two very opposite standpoints. Once again I closed a G.K. Chesterton book with a big smile on my face.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13011794847220170830" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33421267" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33421267" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1547645-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33421267?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/1547645-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33421267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10721743763227758638">Probably the best of Chesterton's fiction that I have read. I think this book may be better than The Man Who Was Thursday. The society outlined in this book looks eerily like our own. The whole world has gone insane, yet it has placed the sane in the asylum. Chesterton does justice to the fact that there can be friendship between disagreeing parties, and this friendship can do more than all the battles to soften hearts and make them receptive to the grace of God.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2313841845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2313841845" />
    <a title="David West" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68766242-david-west"><img alt="David West" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2313841845?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David West" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David West" href="/user/show/68766242-david-west">David West</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2313841845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5997303337259659889">I am giving the book 5 stars because of how brilliantly it is written. Every sentence is an incredible show of vocabulary and description, a joy to read. The story itself seems to go from very literal to symbolic about 2/3rds in. The first literal part I found to be very comic and clever at getting at the ridiculous avenues of relativism and asks the question “what is worth fighting for if not our beliefs?” The second part, the more symbolic part, takes on a slightly darker tone. I liked the end</span>
  <span id="freeText5997303337259659889" style="display:none">I am giving the book 5 stars because of how brilliantly it is written. Every sentence is an incredible show of vocabulary and description, a joy to read. The story itself seems to go from very literal to symbolic about 2/3rds in. The first literal part I found to be very comic and clever at getting at the ridiculous avenues of relativism and asks the question “what is worth fighting for if not our beliefs?” The second part, the more symbolic part, takes on a slightly darker tone. I liked the ending and will need to reflect more on the symbols and meaning of the final third, but overall a great read and one I think would be good to discuss with another person.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5997303337259659889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_250588680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/250588680" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/132592-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182352204p2/132592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/250588680?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/132592-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/132592-matthew?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250588680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8774241624271544194">Bizarre but certainly entertaining and provocative. I'm a big fan of Chesterton with his rollicking plots and writing style and the question is why he is not more widely read today, albeit having a niche group of fans that apparently includes Neil Gaiman and Terry Pratchett. I had a glimmer of the answer in reading his collected Essays -- including one in which he defends the jury as opposed to the single judge system, in the spirit of democracy; his argument, to me, felt dated, although i empat</span>
  <span id="freeText8774241624271544194" style="display:none">Bizarre but certainly entertaining and provocative. I'm a big fan of Chesterton with his rollicking plots and writing style and the question is why he is not more widely read today, albeit having a niche group of fans that apparently includes Neil Gaiman and Terry Pratchett. I had a glimmer of the answer in reading his collected Essays -- including one in which he defends the jury as opposed to the single judge system, in the spirit of democracy; his argument, to me, felt dated, although i empathise with his faith in the common sense of the everyday man and his concern for the abuse of power. The Ball and the Cross also feels a little dated. Chesterton (as well as CS Lewis) appears to have this caricatured conception of modern science as a Nietzschean will to power, and his villain in this case is an abstract polemic made flesh rather than a fully fleshed out character. Then again most of his fictional writing is really metaphysical argument couched as story. Having said that I very much like the balanced and respectful way in which he portrays Turnbull, the humanist atheist, and his slight satire of the humorless MacIan -- this of course despite his personal bias in beliefs toward the latter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8774241624271544194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1566556215" />
    <a title="Manuel Alfonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35970992-manuel-alfonseca"><img alt="Manuel Alfonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420279369p2/35970992.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1566556215?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manuel Alfonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manuel Alfonseca" href="/user/show/35970992-manuel-alfonseca">Manuel Alfonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1566556215" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4849958998801030889">Written one year later, this novel is the counterpoint to "The man who was Thursday", which I read five times many years ago. On the other hand, I have just read once "The ball & the cross". Perhaps this is the reason why I gave five stars to the former, and only four to the latter.<br/><br/>I am not disclosing anything if I say that the main antagonist in this novel represents the Devil, for from the beginning he is called Dr. Lucifer. The main characters are one atheist and a Catholic who pass the whole</span>
  <span id="freeText4849958998801030889" style="display:none">Written one year later, this novel is the counterpoint to "The man who was Thursday", which I read five times many years ago. On the other hand, I have just read once "The ball &amp; the cross". Perhaps this is the reason why I gave five stars to the former, and only four to the latter.<br /><br />I am not disclosing anything if I say that the main antagonist in this novel represents the Devil, for from the beginning he is called Dr. Lucifer. The main characters are one atheist and a Catholic who pass the whole book trying to duel on a matter of convictions and are never allowed to do it. Thus they become the worst enemies of Dr. Lucifer, who cannot tolerate people who are essentially true to their convictions, whatever they are.<br /><br />I should correct the previous assertion: in fact Dr. Lucifer fears one person even more than those two, but I won't disclose who he is and leave the reader to discover it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4849958998801030889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_590860700" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/590860700" />
    <a title="Morris Nelms" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2154411-morris-nelms"><img alt="Morris Nelms" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471342490p2/2154411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/590860700?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Morris Nelms" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Morris Nelms" href="/user/show/2154411-morris-nelms">Morris Nelms</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6353409-the-ball-and-the-cross">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2154411-morris-nelms?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2154411-morris-nelms?shelf=babu">babu</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer590860700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3512576064951922987">This is the first work of fiction by Chesterton I've read. It's a fine book. Elegant and witty writing prevails throughout. Chesterton, a devout Catholic, makes the atheist the most lovable character. <br/>It's funny. It's funny like Twain and Wodehouse are funny. It's also deep at times, often at the same time that it's being funny. <br/><br/>This book may not be for everyone. Some will find it too philosophical. Some will say that Chesterton is engaging in straw man arguments, that he does a poor job present</span>
  <span id="freeText3512576064951922987" style="display:none">This is the first work of fiction by Chesterton I've read. It's a fine book. Elegant and witty writing prevails throughout. Chesterton, a devout Catholic, makes the atheist the most lovable character. <br />It's funny. It's funny like Twain and Wodehouse are funny. It's also deep at times, often at the same time that it's being funny. <br /><br />This book may not be for everyone. Some will find it too philosophical. Some will say that Chesterton is engaging in straw man arguments, that he does a poor job presenting the atheist's side of things, but by the end of it I felt Turnbull's ideas were well represented. And I'm sure some will say it is silly. <br /><br />I say this is an important book and should be widely read. I loved it, and I loved it more than I have loved a new book in quite some time. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3512576064951922987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jacob Stock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22299312-jacob-stock"><img alt="Jacob Stock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516134324p2/22299312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2851364947?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Stock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Stock" href="/user/show/22299312-jacob-stock">Jacob Stock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2851364947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer571447393248408698">Well, I must say I did thoroughly love this read. As it got further into the strangeness of any Chesterton novel, I was all the more excited by the strange but profound turns. I guess I feel so like a poor chap from the story that I too often think I have gone mad in this world believing in the things I do. It was so great at turning my head toward the non-material, the world that is to come. I found that even the submission to the wrong and odd was kind of ok as one longs for the world to come.</span>
  <span id="freeText571447393248408698" style="display:none">Well, I must say I did thoroughly love this read. As it got further into the strangeness of any Chesterton novel, I was all the more excited by the strange but profound turns. I guess I feel so like a poor chap from the story that I too often think I have gone mad in this world believing in the things I do. It was so great at turning my head toward the non-material, the world that is to come. I found that even the submission to the wrong and odd was kind of ok as one longs for the world to come. Judgement and all things will be made right...and somehow even I whom is also wrong will be let into a world of real truth and beauty. Refreshing. Odd. Profound.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17130324121669195059">Many reviewers have called this entire novel a confusing allegory, and even Chesterton himself confessed in later years that he was a bit confused by it. Nevertheless, the story of an atheist and an orthodox Roman Catholic trying to fight a duel over faith, despite continual police intervention, is amusing and interesting. Duels were decidedly uncouth in Edwardian England, as indeed was any public discussion of religion. Religion was a private matter, after all. The climax is a bit more puzzling</span>
  <span id="freeText17130324121669195059" style="display:none">Many reviewers have called this entire novel a confusing allegory, and even Chesterton himself confessed in later years that he was a bit confused by it. Nevertheless, the story of an atheist and an orthodox Roman Catholic trying to fight a duel over faith, despite continual police intervention, is amusing and interesting. Duels were decidedly uncouth in Edwardian England, as indeed was any public discussion of religion. Religion was a private matter, after all. The climax is a bit more puzzling, but Chesterton's wit keeps you engaged throughout the book.</span>

<div id="review_3379860098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3379860098" />
    <a title="James Gailey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38578710-james-gailey"><img alt="James Gailey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572263767p2/38578710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3379860098?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Gailey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Gailey" href="/user/show/38578710-james-gailey">James Gailey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3379860098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4756481482653447269">“I am not at all surprised," he said, "at the world being against us. It makes me feel I was right to..." "Yes?" said Turnbull. "To smash your window," said MacIan. "I have woken up the world.”<br/><br/>It's hard to communicate the gospel with someone who does not believe that God exists. It is much harder to communicate the same message to someone who doesn't care if God exists. We are very familiar with the fierce debates that pit the forces of Christianity and atheism, or religion and irreligion. Howev</span>
  <span id="freeText4756481482653447269" style="display:none">“I am not at all surprised," he said, "at the world being against us. It makes me feel I was right to..." "Yes?" said Turnbull. "To smash your window," said MacIan. "I have woken up the world.”<br /><br />It's hard to communicate the gospel with someone who does not believe that God exists. It is much harder to communicate the same message to someone who doesn't care if God exists. We are very familiar with the fierce debates that pit the forces of Christianity and atheism, or religion and irreligion. However, a more prevalent view encompasses the society we live in - one based on the proposition that such questions should never be asked. Speak YOUR Truth. <br /><br />This is a very modern and important discussion, yet it is the focus of GK Chesterton's The Ball and the Cross, penned over a century ago. This is not surprising considering the wonderful mind of the British journalist and theologian was always fixated on eternal values that continue to resonate across the ages - quite like this book.<br /><br />The Ball and the Cross focus on the conflict between a Christian highlander named McIan and a Scottish skeptic named Turnbull. After a major theological disagreement, both draw swords and agree to a duel to maintain the honor of their tightly held beliefs. The world around them view this quarrel not only as madness but dangerous and stop at nothing to apprehend these radicals before the battle can take place. The two duelists, both willing to die for what they stand for, soon discover that it is this common desire that unites them against the world. <br /><br />The story's apparent message is excellently portrayed through two incredibly well-realized main characters that are equal parts likable and flawed. McIan, the spiritually minded Roman Catholic, is not as brainless as his counterpart suggests, yet his admirable passion for his beliefs often cause him to act in violence before he thinks. The atheistic Turnbull has a growling exterior that he uses to defy Christian tradition at the drop of a hat, but there is a gentleness that can only be found through friendship. Despite being an uncompromising Christian himself, Chesterton gives the skeptic a fair and likable portrayal, which is refreshing. Turnbull's position is clearly critiqued more heavily (and rightly so), but you grow as fond of him as McIan does as the story progresses. I think Christian should follow the example of writing more complex religious and nonreligious characters in their novels.<br /><br />These eccentric characters, along with the many individuals they run into, effectively show a delicate tightrope we must walk between apathy and violent zeal. On one hand, we should never reduce the truths of Christianity as a mere lifestyle choice personal to the individual. The question of if God exists is one in which the answer has deep consequences. The cost of looking like an idiot in an individualistic world is a price worth paying. That said, we have all witnessed how strongly held beliefs can give some an irrational desire to duel to the death, metaphorically or literally. We should not be quick to draw our swords and annihilate anyone who deviates from us. Without compromising the importance of the debate itself, we should look beyond the opposing belief system and value the person who articulates it. <br /><br />In typical Chesterton fashion, the plot frequently halts for long discussions of philosophy and theology that are engaging, if slightly complicated. There are tons of great side-observations on the nature of journalism, modernism, and the power of language that have little impact on the story but are welcome the less. When the plot picks up, however, it takes on a cartoony yet cinematic persona. The host of side characters are larger than life and lead to many genuinely laugh-out-loud moments. To some, the ending may "jump the shark", with the addition of fantasy elements that were mostly absent throughout the book. Truthfully, I appreciated the charm, though recognize it was a little jarring.<br /><br />Some sections of the novel may be lost on modern audiences, with many sections implying a knowledge of the history of Great Britain and France. Also, while I do greatly admire Chesterton, he is occasionally is too high concept, and some of his analogies and discussions seem like he was trying too hard to be profound. Finally, while I was broadly on McIan's side throughout, It was difficult to agree with many of the claims he made throughout the book, being from a reformed position myself. That may be a turn off for many readers of a similar background.<br /><br />I will undoubtedly return to this book in the future. It hits home an important and relevant message with charm and wit. It makes me wish there were more stories that convey Christian truths with engaging characters and fun stories that not just pure allegories. Now onto "The Man Who Was Thursday"!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4756481482653447269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3377566201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3377566201" />
    <a title="The Usual" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56007684-the-usual"><img alt="The Usual" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3377566201?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Usual" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Usual" href="/user/show/56007684-the-usual">The Usual</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3377566201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6326829727951568377">Another very odd novel by G. K. Chesterton, and one that sits somewhere between The Napoleon of Notting Hill and The Man Who Was Thursday in the weirdness stakes. It isn't quite as good as either unfortunately. The first chapter in particular is pretty dire, even if it does set the stage for some of the symbolism, and he conflates a number of different points. I can understand his leaning towards obscurantism in context, but that doesn't mean I have to agree with it. Still, it's very Chesterton </span>
  <span id="freeText6326829727951568377" style="display:none">Another very odd novel by G. K. Chesterton, and one that sits somewhere between The Napoleon of Notting Hill and The Man Who Was Thursday in the weirdness stakes. It isn't quite as good as either unfortunately. The first chapter in particular is pretty dire, even if it does set the stage for some of the symbolism, and he conflates a number of different points. I can understand his leaning towards obscurantism in context, but that doesn't mean I have to agree with it. Still, it's very Chesterton right down to the obsession with red hair, so it's worth reading. <br /><br />Minor point: two symbolically necessary dream-sequences neatly segregated into their own chapters. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6326829727951568377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_181605655" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181605655" />
    <a title="Dave/Maggie Bean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4392309-dave-maggie-bean"><img alt="Dave/Maggie Bean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307702735p2/4392309.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181605655?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave/Maggie Bean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave/Maggie Bean" href="/user/show/4392309-dave-maggie-bean">Dave/Maggie Bean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4392309-dave-maggie-bean?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4392309-dave-maggie-bean?shelf=british-and-irish-lit">british-and-irish-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181605655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13103728054260565274">I purchased the Dover edition, which contains a fantastic foreword by Martin Gardner. Said foreword is worth the price of the book – a pittance at $7.95. The text itself is worth twenty times more. TBATC was purportedly inspired by Chesterton’s debates with (deservedly) forgotten R.P.G. Blatchford. (I doubt even Gardner -- who refers to ciphers and numerology in his foreword-- caught the "woo-woo" significance of Blatchford’s initials: Blatchford, like the Soviets, whose B-40/RPG-7 became a weap</span>
  <span id="freeText13103728054260565274" style="display:none">I purchased the Dover edition, which contains a fantastic foreword by Martin Gardner. Said foreword is worth the price of the book – a pittance at $7.95. The text itself is worth twenty times more. TBATC was purportedly inspired by Chesterton’s debates with (deservedly) forgotten R.P.G. Blatchford. (I doubt even Gardner -- who refers to ciphers and numerology in his foreword-- caught the "woo-woo" significance of Blatchford’s initials: Blatchford, like the Soviets, whose B-40/RPG-7 became a weapon of choice among humorless malcontents the world over, was both a determinist and a socialist.) Chesterton, a "distributist" disliked capitalism and socialism, "conservatism" and "progressivism" equally. Like Charles Dickens, his convictions apparently stemmed from misplaced -- but justifiable -- anti-elitism and genuine humanitarianism. Whereas I’m not completely sold on his "distributist" ideas (how does one ensure that productive property finds its way into as many hands as possible -- without resorting to state-sanctioned theft?) or any other "third way" philosophy, the system Chesterton envisioned was far less brutal than the nasty mix of socialism and fascism infecting most of the world nowadays.<br /><br />Beyond his odd economic theories, Chesterton was an unapologetic and devout Roman Catholic. Needless to say, his faith colored every word-picture he composed, whether blatantly or subtly. The Ball and the Cross was certainly no exception to his general rule. In an interesting variation on the pre-1745 Highland/Lowland, Catholic/Protestant historical theme, Chesterton – an Englishman—creates two worthy adversaries in Evan MacIan and John Turnbull. The former is a tall, dark-haired, blue-eyed Highlander: the latter a short, stocky, red-haired, grey-eyed Lowlander. (And yes, I suspect Chesterton had read Scott’s poem, "Marmion."…)<br /><br />MacIan is a devout, simple, mystic Catholic/Jacobite, longing for the return of the "auld Stuarts." Lowlander Turnbull, however, is *not* a stern, surly, pragmatic Covenanter; but rather a stern, surly, pragmatic atheist, longing to leap forward into a (putatively) rational utopia.<br /><br />To make a long story short, Turnbull denigrates the Virgin Mary. MacIan responds with physical force and a challenge to a duel of honor, which Turnbull willingly accepts. The ensuing (and hilarious) quest to elude the authorities and consummate their engagement makes for one hell of an entertaining novel, and Turnbull’s and MacIan’s arguments – while endlessly amusing -- also provide much food for thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13103728054260565274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1442477632" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1442477632" />
    <a title="Don Incognito" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526509-don-incognito"><img alt="Don Incognito" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254559557p2/1526509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1442477632?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Don Incognito" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Don Incognito" href="/user/show/1526509-don-incognito">Don Incognito</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1442477632&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1442477632" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3111723040764334898">This is a book whose premise, and beginning, you need to be Roman Catholic in order to fully appreciate. Since I'm Protestant, I was befuddled.<br/><br/>In the beginning, the Catholic premise begins after a seemingly irrelevant chapter about an airship designed and flown by a mad scientist. Turnbull, a smug atheist newspaper publisher in London, has apparently published an article insulting the Virgin Mary. MacIan, a militant traditionalist Catholic man from Scotland, reads it and smashes the publisher's </span>
  <span id="freeText3111723040764334898" style="display:none">This is a book whose premise, and beginning, you need to be Roman Catholic in order to fully appreciate. Since I'm Protestant, I was befuddled.<br /><br />In the beginning, the Catholic premise begins after a seemingly irrelevant chapter about an airship designed and flown by a mad scientist. Turnbull, a smug atheist newspaper publisher in London, has apparently published an article insulting the Virgin Mary. MacIan, a militant traditionalist Catholic man from Scotland, reads it and smashes the publisher's window in a rage. He challenges Turnbull to a duel; Turnbull accepts; and after buying swords in an antique shop, they have at it, fully attempting to kill each other. I was unable to sympathize with wanting to murder someone for insulting one's religion.<br /><br />What happens for about the next two-thirds of the book is almost a screwball comedy: MacIan and Turnbull, who become almost friends, keep going from place to place for their duel because people keep interrupting them and the police keep finding and chasing them.<br /><br />The story eventually becomes more serious, and takes a bizarre and much more interesting turn. It becomes, in fact, possibly the strangest Chesterton book I've yet read. (The others being The Napoleon of Notting Hill; Manalive; The Man Who Was Thursday; The Everlasting Man; and What I Saw In America.) Even The Man Who Was Thursday doesn't seem so absurd, once you accept that it's surreal.<br /><br />MacIan and Turnbull jump over a wall to evade the latest police pursuit, and eventually find that they have been tricked into entering an inescapable insane asylum that was apparently designed by mysterious and evil people who may or may not be trying to take over England. This section, very much like The Man Who Was Thursday, is so strange and surreal (if definitely less puzzling and difficult) that I have felt compelled to reread it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3111723040764334898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2197579666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15871014004631919698">Having read and enjoyed "The Man Who Was Thursday," and having discovered this book thanks to my devotion to (now) Venerable John Paul I, who comments on it in his Illustrissimi (which I highly recommend), I thought I would give it a read.<br/><br/>I didn't know what to expect, except that Chesterton is a master of words and his allegory is often confusing. I greatly enjoyed the roller coaster that was this book, just as I enjoyed "The Man Who Was Thursday." The journey is long and often puzzling, seeming</span>
  <span id="freeText15871014004631919698" style="display:none">Having read and enjoyed "The Man Who Was Thursday," and having discovered this book thanks to my devotion to (now) Venerable John Paul I, who comments on it in his Illustrissimi (which I highly recommend), I thought I would give it a read.<br /><br />I didn't know what to expect, except that Chesterton is a master of words and his allegory is often confusing. I greatly enjoyed the roller coaster that was this book, just as I enjoyed "The Man Who Was Thursday." The journey is long and often puzzling, seeming to aimlessly wander. He seems to like making the reader fix on a single moment of destination and dance around it for the entirely of the book, often never quite reaching that moment. And yet I don't feel unsatisfied by it.<br /><br />I greatly appreciated the contrast between the two main characters. They are so apparently different, often violently so, and yet there is something which unites them both immensely - a character which is rarely found in today's world. I think Chesterton here hits on a valuable point - often those who disagree on an issue or have opinions which are polar opposites ought to be closest friends because of their similar attitudes and virtues. Just as love and hatred are considered opposites, in reality it is said that love and apathy are much further apart, and love is one step away from hatred.<br /><br />I didn't understand or appreciate all of the allegory and symbolism in the book, although I suspect a more careful reading would unlock some of that for me. But I thoroughly enjoyed Chesterton's random musings and his philosophy hidden in narrative. In particular, he has a way of showing something upside-down as being right-side-up and then slowly revealing the truth of the situation. And he does this while still maintaining a high level of action and suspense.<br /><br />I loved it, just as I've loved all of his works which I have read so far. Enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15871014004631919698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_486104984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/486104984" />
    <a title="Joel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8011435-joel"><img alt="Joel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346647344p2/8011435.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/486104984?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joel" href="/user/show/8011435-joel">Joel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer486104984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2922353971645105826">This book is not about the everyday world. It has little in common with the modern novels I am used to. It is not interested in exploring the nuances of introspection and social interaction. It seemed to me to be more like a farce with characters running around implausibly, saying witty, profound things. It also felt at times like a morality play with characters declaring their views on the state of the universe and of each other.<br/><br/>In the end, it is its own sort of book, a book obsessed not with "</span>
  <span id="freeText2922353971645105826" style="display:none">This book is not about the everyday world. It has little in common with the modern novels I am used to. It is not interested in exploring the nuances of introspection and social interaction. It seemed to me to be more like a farce with characters running around implausibly, saying witty, profound things. It also felt at times like a morality play with characters declaring their views on the state of the universe and of each other.<br /><br />In the end, it is its own sort of book, a book obsessed not with "real life" but with reality. That is, it is a book interested in those parts of life that seem the most real, the parts when you are falling in love or escaping death or taking a stand for what is right. An absurd number of scenes take place at sunset or sunrise, with the almost automatic beauty and poignancy those times can carry.<br /><br /><i>The Ball and The Cross</i> is about a devout Catholic and dogmatic atheist whose passionate stand against each other set them apart from the world and thus throws them together. At least, that is what the first half of the book is about. The second half (or maybe final third) is something else altogether—a Kafkaesque nightmare leading to a redemptive apocalypse, maybe. It's a little hard to describe.<br /><br />Although <i>The Ball and The Cross</i> can feel didactic at times (it has no postmodern ambiguity about where it lands on the theism/atheism debate, for example), it is like little else you can read. If you can accept that G. K. Chesterton is doing his own thing here, the hyper-symbolic weirdness of its ending is really amazing.<br /><br />NOTE: If you liked this book even a little, I would highly recommend <i>Napoleon of Notting Hill</i>. It has a similar narrative arc and is my very favorite of Chesterton's novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2922353971645105826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58104602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7996886579396825009">I just finished rereading The Ball and The Cross. It's not a very good novel--Chesterton himself did not like how it turned out--but I enjoyed it--it was at least a good idea for a story, and expresses, if poorly, some very fun and interesting ideas. I also reread Martin Gardner's introduction to the book and thought it was not very good. Contrary to what he says, the novel isn't really about the conflict between the Catholic and the atheist--it's about what brings them together--that specifical</span>
  <span id="freeText7996886579396825009" style="display:none">I just finished rereading The Ball and The Cross. It's not a very good novel--Chesterton himself did not like how it turned out--but I enjoyed it--it was at least a good idea for a story, and expresses, if poorly, some very fun and interesting ideas. I also reread Martin Gardner's introduction to the book and thought it was not very good. Contrary to what he says, the novel isn't really about the conflict between the Catholic and the atheist--it's about what brings them together--that specifically being a world that hates them both. But then, as it turns out, it's not really the world that hates them but the devil. The world is just a bit misguided.<br /><br />Although Chesterton would surely blanche at the suggestion that he was a universalist, he seemed to think so well of everyone he met, I doubt that he could really imagine any person being worthy of eternal damnation. And that's played out in the ending of the book, which makes it rather a curious statement for someone so concerned with "orthdoxy."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7996886579396825009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6698864492601506470">November 1, 2016<br/>A Review by Anthony T. Riggio of the Book "The Ball and the Cross" by G.K. Chesterton<br/><br/>I purchased this (free) book in Kindle format from Amazon. I have heard a lot about Chesterton and his religious/philosophical views and was curious about his writing style. This book, The Ball and the Cross is an allegorical story showing the conflicts between good and evil and uses the relationship that develop a philosophical distain for one another's beliefs or lack thereof. The believer chal</span>
  <span id="freeText6698864492601506470" style="display:none">November 1, 2016<br />A Review by Anthony T. Riggio of the Book "The Ball and the Cross" by G.K. Chesterton<br /><br />I purchased this (free) book in Kindle format from Amazon. I have heard a lot about Chesterton and his religious/philosophical views and was curious about his writing style. This book, The Ball and the Cross is an allegorical story showing the conflicts between good and evil and uses the relationship that develop a philosophical distain for one another's beliefs or lack thereof. The believer challenges the disbeliever to a dual using swords. The story is comedic and reminiscent of Cervantes Don Quixote. It sets out a series of humorist adventures which follow in a consequential sequence. I believe it sets out a struggle for the author and as a consequence demonstrates his belief system. I liked the book but it was a challenge to read to keep up with the whimsy of the author and somewhat circuitous in his overall conclusion. I gave the book three stars, primarily because it was interesting but lacking in a flow that appealed to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6698864492601506470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer303229134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2980926185720124689">Delightful and thought provoking as ever. Highly recommended. However, I think it is best appreciated when there is a familiarity with some of Chesterton's other works in general and ORTHODOXY in particular. I saw many ideas and settings from ORTHODOXY which actually took place in this book. Insane asylums, the men who discover England, what things are worth fighting for, believing in oneself... all shared by both ORTHODOXY and THE BALL AND THE CROSS. They really need to be read together.<br/><br/>Someone</span>
  <span id="freeText2980926185720124689" style="display:none">Delightful and thought provoking as ever. Highly recommended. However, I think it is best appreciated when there is a familiarity with some of Chesterton's other works in general and ORTHODOXY in particular. I saw many ideas and settings from ORTHODOXY which actually took place in this book. Insane asylums, the men who discover England, what things are worth fighting for, believing in oneself... all shared by both ORTHODOXY and THE BALL AND THE CROSS. They really need to be read together.<br /><br />Someone else said it, and I'll badly paraphrase it, but it rings true through all of Chesterton's works: When you read Chesterton, the characters are never convincing, never "real," nobody in the real world is ever truly like a Chesterton character. But as you read, you begin to play peek-a-boo with an idea: That this is perhaps the way people ought to be.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2980926185720124689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qC1SzlyWPu1g4Hrc1Q+6aEggKtxkyVUF+kDCzzn64s9wCPvn3Jesj0Wxe3L6gLpdF/rmDKhQ5cNWPCOm9sP3YA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3Qtu04jtr8LnIa5WdX29kqXkNJWzvrOr1qUazakJKi4FLsf6COw9oMJwr/ha8r2n+j74RX8nA2162fukZjA/gQ==')})
  }

In it, the orphaned Fran<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8514193984461982600\" style=\"display:none\">First published in 1960, The Violent Bear It Away is now a landmark in American literature. It is a dark and absorbing example of the Gothic sensibility and bracing satirical voice that are united in Flannery O\'Conner\'s work. In it, the orphaned Francis Marion Tarwater and his cousins, the schoolteacher Rayber, defy the prophecy of their dead uncle--that Tarwater will become a prophet and will baptize Rayber\'s young son, Bishop. A series of struggles ensues: Tarwater fights an internal battle against his innate faith and the voices calling him to be a prophet while Rayber tries to draw Tarwater into a more &quot;reasonable&quot; modern world. Both wrestle with the legacy of their dead relatives and lay claim to Bishop\'s soul.O\'Connor observes all this with an astonishing combination of irony and compassion, humor and pathos, resulting in a novel where range and depth reveal a brilliant and innovative writers acutely alert to where the sacred lives and to where it does not.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8514193984461982600\" href=\"#\" He was a gifted journalist, essayist, biographer, poet, novelist, playwright, philosopher, debater, and defender of common sense, of Christianity, and of the Catholic faith. He was truly an influential man of his time, writing thousands of essays and hundreds of books. Today he remains one of the best and most quoted writers of the English language. In this book of essays, Father James V. Schall, a prolific author himself and a prominent Catholic writer, brings readers to Chesterton through a witty series of original reflections prompted by something Chesterton wrote--timely essays on timeless issues. Like Chesterton, Schall consciously leads the reader to the reality of what is, of what is true and what is at the heart of things. It is a handbook of how to take up almost any essay or chapter or paragraph of Chesterton\'s many works and, upon further reflection, come to realize that he was a profoundly wise man who still teaches vividly and accurately a century after he wrote. Schall easily captures Chesterton\'s fondness of life and laughter, and at the same time, makes readers aware of Chesterton\'s extreme insight and rigorous understanding of ideas and truth.\n\nIncluded in this book is an introductory chapter on Chesterton as a &quot;journalist,&quot; which is how he identified himself, and a concluding chapter that provides an extended reflection on Chesterton\'s world. Forty-one essays comprise the heart of the book. They range widely in subject matter, from the Catholic Church as the &quot;natural home of the human spirit,&quot; through such topics as virtue and honor, horror and detective stories, toys and Christmas, right and wrong, to the shocking conclusion that indeed &quot;dogmas are not dull.&quot;\n\nJames V. Schall, S.J., is author of more than twenty books, hundreds of articles, and monthly columns in Gilbert! and Crisis. He is professor in the Department of Government at Georgetown University. His book At the Limits of Political Philosophy: From &quot;Brilliant Errors&quot; to Things of Uncommon Importance was published by CUA Press in 1996.\n\nPRAISE FOR THE BOOK: &quot;Schall on Chesterton sends us rushing back to Chesterton\'s own writings with new insights and renewed enthusiasm. It is the guide to the twentieth century\'s wisest and most misunderstood prophet.&quot;--John Peterson, editor, Gilbert! &quot;One of the great themes in Father Schall\'s book derives from his insistance that good literature provide a moral illumination for ordinary life. Because of the vast number of books and articles which Chesterton wrote, few people can claim and exhaustive knowledge of his writings. Father Schall is one of that small company. He shares with his hero something that Chesterton attributed to St. Thomas Aquinas--an intense interest in the significance of everyday existence, a quality which Chesterton called &quot;a fury for life.\' &quot;--Rev. Ian Boyd, C.S.B., editor, The Chesterton Review\n\n&quot;Who could be more appropriate to write about Chesterton than so subtle and prolific an essayist as Father James V. Schall? Like Chesterton, he is a skilled presenter of eternal truths.&quot;--Prof. John P. McCarthy, Fordham University&quot;Father James Schall excels as an essayist whose critical discriminations and insights are invaluable to readers in search of literary and political and religious understanding of the more vexing problems of the modern world.&quot;--Prof. George A.
